Microsoft co-founder&nbsp;<a title="Paul Allen" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Allen">Paul Allen</a>&nbsp;believed that such intelligence is unlikely in the 21st century because it would require "unforeseeable and fundamentally unpredictable breakthroughs" and a "scientifically deep understanding of cognition".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup>&nbsp;Writing in&nbsp;<em><a title="The Guardian" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Guardian">The Guardian</a></em>, roboticist Alan Winfield claimed the gulf between modern computing and human-level artificial intelligence is as wide as the gulf between current space flight and practical faster-than-light spaceflight.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most AI researchers believe strong AI can be achieved in the future, but some thinkers, like&nbsp;<a title="Hubert Dreyfus" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus">Hubert Dreyfus</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Roger Penrose" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Penrose">Roger Penrose</a>, deny the possibility of achieving strong AI.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003_79-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003-79">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_80-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:0-80">[76]</a></sup>&nbsp;<a title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)">John McCarthy</a>&nbsp;is among those who believe human-level AI will be accomplished, but that the present level of progress is such that a date cannot accurately be predicted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy2007_81-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthy2007-81">[77]</a></sup>&nbsp;AI experts' views on the feasibility of AGI wax and wane. Four polls conducted in 2012 and 2013 suggested that the median guess among experts for when they would be 50% confident AGI would arrive was 2040 to 2050, depending on the poll, with the mean being 2081. Of the experts, 16.5% answered with "never" when asked the same question but with a 90% confidence instead.<sup id="cite_ref-new_yorker_doomsday_82-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-new_yorker_doomsday-82">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup>&nbsp;Further current AGI progress considerations can be found above&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#Tests_for_confirming_human-level_AGI"><em>Tests for confirming human-level AGI</em></a>.</p>
<p>A report by Stuart Armstrong and Kaj Sotala of the Machine Intelligence Research Institute found that "over [a] 60 year time frame there is a strong bias towards predicting the arrival of human level AI as between 15 and 25 years from the time the prediction was made". They analyzed 95 predictions made between 1950 and 2012 on when human level AI will come about.&nbsp;<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Timescales" class="mw-headline">Timescales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Timescales" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the introduction to his 2006 book,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoertzelPennachin2006_85-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoertzelPennachin2006-85">[81]</a></sup>&nbsp;Goertzel says that estimates of the time needed before a truly flexible AGI is built vary from 10 years to over a century. As of 2007&nbsp;the consensus in the AGI research community seemed to be that the timeline discussed by&nbsp;<a title="Ray Kurzweil" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a>&nbsp;in&nbsp;<em><a class="mw-redirect" title="The Singularity is Near" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Singularity_is_Near">The Singularity is Near</a></em><sup id="cite_ref-K_86-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-K-86">[82]</a></sup>&nbsp;(i.e. between 2015 and 2045) was plausible.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007_87-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007-87">[83]</a></sup>&nbsp;Mainstream AI researchers have given a wide range of opinions on whether progress will be this rapid. A 2012 meta-analysis of 95 such opinions found a bias towards predicting that the onset of AGI would occur within 16&ndash;26 years for modern and historical predictions alike. That paper has been criticized for how it categorized opinions as expert or non-expert.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2012,&nbsp;<a title="Alex Krizhevsky" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alex_Krizhevsky">Alex Krizhevsky</a>,&nbsp;<a title="Ilya Sutskever" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilya_Sutskever">Ilya Sutskever</a>, and&nbsp;<a title="Geoffrey Hinton" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton">Geoffrey Hinton</a>&nbsp;developed a neural network called&nbsp;<a title="AlexNet" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AlexNet">AlexNet</a>, which won the&nbsp;<a title="ImageNet" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ImageNet">ImageNet</a>&nbsp;competition with a top-5 test error rate of 15.3%, significantly better than the second-best entry's rate of 26.3% (the traditional approach used a weighted sum of scores from different pre-defined classifiers).<sup id="cite_ref-:5_89-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:5-89">[85]</a></sup>&nbsp;AlexNet was regarded as the initial ground-breaker of the current deep learning wave.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_89-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:5-89">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2017, researchers Feng Liu, Yong Shi, and Ying Liu conducted intelligence tests on publicly available and freely accessible weak AI such as Google AI, Apple's Siri, and others. At the maximum, these AIs reached an IQ value of about 47, which corresponds approximately to a six-year-old child in first grade. An adult comes to about 100 on average. Similar tests were carried out in 2014, with the IQ score reaching a maximum value of 27.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2020,&nbsp;<a title="OpenAI" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenAI">OpenAI</a>&nbsp;developed&nbsp;<a title="GPT-3" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPT-3">GPT-3</a>, a language model capable of performing many diverse tasks without specific training. According to&nbsp;<a title="Gary Grossman" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gary_Grossman">Gary Grossman</a>&nbsp;in a&nbsp;<a title="VentureBeat" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/VentureBeat">VentureBeat</a>&nbsp;article, while there is consensus that GPT-3 is not an example of AGI, it is considered by some to be too advanced to classify as a narrow AI system.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the same year, Jason Rohrer used his GPT-3 account to develop a chatbot, and provided a chatbot-developing platform called "Project December". OpenAI asked for changes to the chatbot to comply with their safety guidelines; Rohrer disconnected Project December from the GPT-3 API.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2022,&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="DeepMind" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DeepMind">DeepMind</a>&nbsp;developed&nbsp;<a title="Gato (DeepMind)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gato_(DeepMind)">Gato</a>, a "general-purpose" system capable of performing more than 600 different tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2023,&nbsp;<a title="Microsoft Research" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microsoft_Research">Microsoft Research</a>&nbsp;published a study on an early version of OpenAI's&nbsp;<a title="GPT-4" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GPT-4">GPT-4</a>, contending that it exhibited more general intelligence than previous AI models and demonstrated human-level performance in tasks spanning multiple domains, such as mathematics, coding, and law. This research sparked a debate on whether GPT-4 could be considered an early, incomplete version of artificial general intelligence, emphasizing the need for further exploration and evaluation of such systems.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2023, the AI researcher&nbsp;<a title="Geoffrey Hinton" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geoffrey_Hinton">Geoffrey Hinton</a>&nbsp;stated that:<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The idea that this stuff could actually get smarter than people &mdash; a few people believed that, [...]. But most people thought it was way off. And I thought it was way off. I thought it was 30 to 50 years or even longer away. Obviously, I no longer think that.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span id="Brain_simulation" class="mw-headline">Brain simulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Brain simulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Whole_brain_emulation" class="mw-headline">Whole brain emulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Whole brain emulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article:&nbsp;<a title="Mind uploading" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind_uploading">Mind uploading</a></div>
<p>One possible approach to achieving AGI is&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Whole brain emulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whole_brain_emulation">whole brain emulation</a>: A brain model is built by&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Brain scanning" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain_scanning">scanning</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Brain mapping" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain_mapping">mapping</a>&nbsp;a biological brain in detail and copying its state into a computer system or another computational device. The computer runs a&nbsp;<a title="Computer simulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_simulation">simulation</a>&nbsp;model sufficiently faithful to the original that it behaves in practically the same way as the original brain.<sup id="cite_ref-Roadmap_97-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-Roadmap-97">[93]</a></sup>&nbsp;Whole brain emulation is discussed in&nbsp;<a title="Computational neuroscience" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_neuroscience">computational neuroscience</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Neuroinformatics" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuroinformatics">neuroinformatics</a>, in the context of&nbsp;<a title="Brain simulation" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain_simulation">brain simulation</a>&nbsp;for medical research purposes. It is discussed in&nbsp;<a title="Artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>&nbsp;research<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007_87-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007-87">[83]</a></sup>&nbsp;as an approach to strong AI.&nbsp;<a title="Neuroimaging" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuroimaging">Neuroimaging</a>&nbsp;technologies that could deliver the necessary detailed understanding are improving rapidly, and&nbsp;<a title="Futurist" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Futurist">futurist</a>&nbsp;<a title="Ray Kurzweil" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a>&nbsp;in the book&nbsp;<em>The Singularity Is Near</em><sup id="cite_ref-K_86-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-K-86">[82]</a></sup>&nbsp;predicts that a map of sufficient quality will become available on a similar timescale to the computing power required to emulate it.</p>
<h3><span id="Early_estimates" class="mw-headline">Early estimates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Early estimates" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner"><a class="image" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg"><img class="thumbimage" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg/478px-Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg/717px-Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg/956px-Estimations_of_Human_Brain_Emulation_Required_Performance.svg.png 2x" width="478" height="359" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify">&nbsp;</div>
Estimates of how much processing power is needed to emulate a human brain at various levels (from Ray Kurzweil,&nbsp;<a title="Anders Sandberg" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anders_Sandberg">Anders Sandberg</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Nick Bostrom" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a>), along with the fastest supercomputer from&nbsp;<a title="TOP500" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TOP500">TOP500</a>&nbsp;mapped by year. Note the logarithmic scale and exponential trendline, which assumes the computational capacity doubles every 1.1 years. Kurzweil believes that mind uploading will be possible at neural simulation, while the Sandberg, Bostrom report is less certain about where&nbsp;<a title="Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness">consciousness</a>&nbsp;arises.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESandbergBostr&ouml;m2008_98-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESandbergBostr%C3%B6m2008-98">[94]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>For low-level brain simulation, an extremely powerful computer would be required. The&nbsp;<a title="Human brain" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_brain">human brain</a>&nbsp;has a huge number of&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Synapses" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Synapses">synapses</a>. Each of the 10<sup>11</sup>&nbsp;(one hundred billion)&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Neurons" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurons">neurons</a>&nbsp;has on average 7,000 synaptic connections (synapses) to other neurons. The brain of a three-year-old child has about 10<sup>15</sup>&nbsp;synapses (1 quadrillion). This number declines with age, stabilizing by adulthood. Estimates vary for an adult, ranging from 10<sup>14</sup>&nbsp;to 5&times;10<sup>14</sup>&nbsp;synapses (100 to 500 trillion).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrachman2005_99-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrachman2005-99">[95]</a></sup>&nbsp;An estimate of the brain's processing power, based on a simple switch model for neuron activity, is around 10<sup>14</sup>&nbsp;(100 trillion) synaptic updates per second (<a title="SUPS" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SUPS">SUPS</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003_100-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003-100">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1997, Kurzweil looked at various estimates for the hardware required to equal the human brain and adopted a figure of 10<sup>16</sup>&nbsp;computations per second (cps).<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-103">[e]</a></sup>&nbsp;(For comparison, if a "computation" was equivalent to one "<a title="FLOPS" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FLOPS">floating-point operation</a>" &ndash; a measure used to rate current&nbsp;<a title="Supercomputer" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supercomputer">supercomputers</a>&nbsp;&ndash; then 10<sup>16</sup>&nbsp;"computations" would be equivalent to 10&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Peta-" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peta-">petaFLOPS</a>,&nbsp;<a title="FLOPS" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FLOPS#Performance_records">achieved in 2011</a>, while 10<sup>18</sup>&nbsp;was&nbsp;<a title="Exascale computing" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exascale_computing">achieved in 2022</a>.) He used this figure to predict the necessary hardware would be available sometime between 2015 and 2025, if the exponential growth in computer power at the time of writing continued.</p>
<h3><span id="Modelling_the_neurons_in_more_detail" class="mw-headline">Modelling the neurons in more detail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Modelling the neurons in more detail" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The&nbsp;<a title="Artificial neuron" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neuron">artificial neuron</a>&nbsp;model assumed by Kurzweil and used in many current&nbsp;<a title="Artificial neural network" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_network">artificial neural network</a>&nbsp;implementations is simple compared with&nbsp;<a title="Biological neuron model" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_neuron_model">biological neurons</a>. A brain simulation would likely have to capture the detailed cellular behaviour of biological&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Neurons" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurons">neurons</a>, presently understood only in broad outline. The overhead introduced by full modeling of the biological, chemical, and physical details of neural behaviour (especially on a molecular scale) would require computational powers several orders of magnitude larger than Kurzweil's estimate. In addition, the estimates do not account for&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Glial cells" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glial_cells">glial cells</a>, which are known to play a role in cognitive processes.<sup id="cite_ref-Discover2011JanFeb_104-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-Discover2011JanFeb-104">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Current_research" class="mw-headline">Current research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Current research" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some research projects are investigating brain simulation using more sophisticated neural models, implemented on conventional computing architectures. The&nbsp;<a title="Artificial Intelligence System" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_Intelligence_System">Artificial Intelligence System</a>&nbsp;project implemented non-real time simulations of a "brain" (with 10<sup>11</sup>&nbsp;neurons) in 2005. It took 50 days on a cluster of 27 processors to simulate 1 second of a model.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup>&nbsp;The&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Brain" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Brain">Blue Brain</a>&nbsp;project used one of the fastest supercomputer architectures,&nbsp;<a title="IBM" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM">IBM</a>'s&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Gene" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Gene">Blue Gene</a>&nbsp;platform, to create a real time simulation of a single rat&nbsp;<a title="Neocortex" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neocortex">neocortical column</a>&nbsp;consisting of approximately 10,000 neurons and 10<sup>8</sup>&nbsp;synapses in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup>&nbsp;A longer-term goal is to build a detailed, functional simulation of the physiological processes in the human brain: "It is not impossible to build a human brain and we can do it in 10 years,"&nbsp;<a title="Henry Markram" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henry_Markram">Henry Markram</a>, director of the Blue Brain Project, said in 2009 at the&nbsp;<a title="TED (conference)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/TED_(conference)">TED conference</a>&nbsp;in Oxford.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup>&nbsp;Neuro-silicon interfaces have been proposed as an alternative implementation strategy that may scale better.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p><a title="Hans Moravec" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Moravec">Hans Moravec</a>&nbsp;addressed the above arguments ("brains are more complicated", "neurons have to be modeled in more detail") in his 1997 paper "When will computer hardware match the human brain?".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoravec1998_102-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec1998-102">[98]</a></sup>&nbsp;He measured the ability of existing software to simulate the functionality of neural tissue, specifically the retina. His results<sup class="noprint Inline-Template">[<em><a title="Wikipedia:Citing sources" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (April 2023)">specify</span></a></em>]</sup>&nbsp;do not depend on the number of glial cells, nor on what kinds of processing neurons perform where.</p>
<p>The actual complexity of modeling biological neurons has been explored in&nbsp;<a title="OpenWorm" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OpenWorm">OpenWorm project</a>&nbsp;that aimed at complete simulation of a worm that has only 302 neurons in its neural network (among about 1000 cells in total). The animal's neural network was well documented before the start of the project. However, although the task seemed simple at the beginning, the models based on a generic neural network did not work. Currently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact">[<em><a title="Portal:Current events" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portal:Current_events"><span title="This statement refers to current events and needs references to newer reliable sources. (April 2023)">may be outdated as of April 2023</span></a></em>]</sup>&nbsp;efforts focus on precise emulation of biological neurons (partly on the molecular level), but the result cannot yet be called a total success.</p>
<h3><span id="Criticisms_of_simulation-based_approaches" class="mw-headline">Criticisms of simulation-based approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Criticisms of simulation-based approaches" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A fundamental criticism of the simulated brain approach derives from&nbsp;<a title="Embodied cognition" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embodied_cognition">embodied cognition</a>&nbsp;theory which asserts that human embodiment is an essential aspect of human intelligence and is necessary to ground meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup>&nbsp;If this theory is correct, any fully functional brain model will need to encompass more than just the neurons (e.g., a robotic body). Goertzel<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007_87-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoertzel2007-87">[83]</a></sup>&nbsp;proposes virtual embodiment (like in&nbsp;<em><a title="Second Life" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Life">Second Life</a></em>) as an option, but it is unknown whether this would be sufficient.</p>
<p>Desktop computers using microprocessors capable of more than 10<sup>9</sup>&nbsp;cps (Kurzweil's non-standard unit "computations per second", see above) have been available since 2005. According to the brain power estimates used by Kurzweil (and Moravec), such a computer should be capable of supporting a simulation of a bee brain, but despite some interest<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup>&nbsp;no such simulation exists.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact">[<em><a title="Wikipedia:Citation needed" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></em>]</sup>&nbsp;There are several reasons for this:</p>
<ol>
<li>The neuron model seems to be oversimplified (see next section).</li>
<li>There is insufficient understanding of higher cognitive processes<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-112">[f]</a></sup>&nbsp;to establish accurately what the brain's neural activity (observed using techniques such as&nbsp;<a title="Neuroimaging" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuroimaging#Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging">functional magnetic resonance imaging</a>) correlates with.</li>
<li>Even if our understanding of cognition advances sufficiently, early simulation programs are likely to be very inefficient and will, therefore, need considerably more hardware.</li>
<li>The brain of an organism, while critical, may not be an appropriate boundary for a cognitive model. To simulate a bee brain, it may be necessary to simulate the body, and the environment.&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="The Extended Mind" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Extended_Mind">The Extended Mind</a>&nbsp;thesis formalises this philosophical concept, and research into&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Cephalopoda" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cephalopoda">cephalopods</a>&nbsp;demonstrated clear examples of a decentralized system.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>In addition, the scale of the human brain is not currently well-constrained. One estimate puts the human brain at about 100 billion neurons and 100 trillion synapses.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup>&nbsp;Another estimate is 86 billion neurons of which 16.3 billion are in the&nbsp;<a title="Cerebral cortex" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebral_cortex">cerebral cortex</a>&nbsp;and 69 billion in the&nbsp;<a title="Cerebellum" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebellum">cerebellum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAzevedo_et_al.2009_116-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAzevedo_et_al.2009-116">[110]</a></sup>&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Glial cell" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glial_cell">Glial cell</a>&nbsp;synapses are currently unquantified but are known to be extremely numerous.</p>
<h2><span id="Philosophical_perspective" class="mw-headline">Philosophical perspective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Philosophical perspective" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also:&nbsp;<a title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence">Philosophy of artificial intelligence</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Turing test" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_test">Turing test</a></div>
<h3><span id=".22Strong_AI.22_as_defined_in_philosophy"></span><span id="&quot;Strong_AI&quot;_as_defined_in_philosophy" class="mw-headline">"Strong AI" as defined in philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: &quot;Strong AI&quot; as defined in philosophy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1980, philosopher&nbsp;<a title="John Searle" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Searle">John Searle</a>&nbsp;coined the term "strong AI" as part of his&nbsp;<a title="Chinese room" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room">Chinese room</a>&nbsp;argument.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup>&nbsp;He wanted to distinguish between two different hypotheses about artificial intelligence:<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-118">[g]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Strong AI hypothesis</strong>: An artificial intelligence system can "think"&mdash;have "a mind" and "consciousness".</li>
<li><strong>Weak AI hypothesis</strong>: An artificial intelligence system can (only)&nbsp;<em>act like</em>&nbsp;it thinks and has a mind and consciousness.</li>
</ul>
<p>The first one he called "strong" because it makes a&nbsp;<em>stronger</em>&nbsp;statement: it assumes something special has happened to the machine that goes beyond those abilities that we can test. The behaviour of a "weak AI" machine would be precisely identical to a "strong AI" machine, but the latter would also have subjective conscious experience. This usage is also common in academic AI research and textbooks.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-119">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mainstream AI is most interested in how a program&nbsp;<em>behaves</em>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-120">[113]</a></sup>&nbsp;According to&nbsp;<a title="Stuart J. Russell" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stuart_J._Russell">Russell</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Peter Norvig" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peter_Norvig">Norvig</a>, "as long as the program works, they don't care if you call it real or a simulation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947_121-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947-121">[114]</a></sup>&nbsp;If the program can behave&nbsp;<em>as if</em>&nbsp;it has a mind, then there is no need to know if it&nbsp;<em>actually</em>&nbsp;has mind &ndash; indeed, there would be no way to tell. For AI research, Searle's "weak AI hypothesis" is equivalent to the statement "artificial general intelligence is possible". Thus, according to Russell and Norvig, "most AI researchers take the weak AI hypothesis for granted, and don't care about the strong AI hypothesis."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947_121-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947-121">[114]</a></sup>&nbsp;Thus, for academic AI research, "Strong AI" and "AGI" are two very different things.</p>
<p>In contrast to Searle and mainstream AI, some futurists such as&nbsp;<a title="Ray Kurzweil" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Kurzweil">Ray Kurzweil</a>&nbsp;use the term "strong AI" to mean "human level artificial general intelligence".<sup id="cite_ref-K_86-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-K-86">[82]</a></sup>&nbsp;This is not the same as Searle's&nbsp;<a class="mw-disambig" title="Strong AI" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strong_AI">strong AI</a>, unless you assume that&nbsp;<a title="Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness">consciousness</a>&nbsp;is necessary for human-level AGI. Academic philosophers such as Searle do not believe that is the case, and to most artificial intelligence researchers the question is out-of-scope.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947_121-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003947-121">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Consciousness" class="mw-headline">Consciousness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Other aspects of the human mind besides intelligence are relevant to the concept of strong AI, and these play a major role in&nbsp;<a title="Science fiction" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science_fiction">science fiction</a>&nbsp;and the&nbsp;<a title="Ethics of artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence">ethics of artificial intelligence</a>:</p>
<ul>
<li><a title="Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness">consciousness</a>: To have&nbsp;<a title="Qualia" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qualia">subjective experience</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="Thought" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought">thought</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-123">[h]</a></sup></li>
<li><a title="Self-awareness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-awareness">self-awareness</a>: To be aware of oneself as a separate individual, especially to be aware of one's own thoughts.</li>
<li><a title="Sentience" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sentience">sentience</a>: The ability to "feel" perceptions or emotions subjectively.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" title="Sapience" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sapience">sapience</a>: The capacity for wisdom.</li>
</ul>
<p>These traits have a moral dimension, because a machine with this form of strong AI may have rights, analogous to the&nbsp;<a title="Animal rights" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_rights">rights of non-human animals</a>. Preliminary work has been conducted on integrating&nbsp;<span class="cleanup-needed-content">full ethical agents</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template">[<em><a title="Wikipedia:Please clarify" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2023)">clarification needed</span></a></em>]</sup>&nbsp;with existing legal and social frameworks, focusing on the legal position and rights of 'strong' AI.<sup id="cite_ref-physica_scripta_124-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-physica_scripta-124">[116]</a></sup>&nbsp;<a title="Bill Joy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Joy">Bill Joy</a>, among others, argues a machine with these traits may be a threat to human life or dignity.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>It remains to be shown whether any of these traits are&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Necessary and sufficient condition" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necessary_and_sufficient_condition">necessary</a>&nbsp;for strong AI. The role of&nbsp;<a title="Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness">consciousness</a>&nbsp;is not clear, and there is no agreed test for its presence. If a machine is built with a device that simulates the&nbsp;<a title="Neural correlates of consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neural_correlates_of_consciousness">neural correlates of consciousness</a>, would it automatically have self-awareness? It is possible that some of these traits&nbsp;<a title="Emergence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emergence">naturally emerge</a>&nbsp;from a fully intelligent machine. It is also possible that people will&nbsp;<em>ascribe</em>&nbsp;these properties to machines once they begin to act in a way that is clearly intelligent.</p>
<h3><span id="Artificial_consciousness_research" class="mw-headline">Artificial consciousness research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Artificial consciousness research" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article:&nbsp;<a title="Artificial consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_consciousness">Artificial consciousness</a></div>
<p>Although the role of consciousness in strong AI/AGI is debatable, many AGI researchers<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYudkowsky2006_111-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYudkowsky2006-111">[106]</a></sup>&nbsp;regard research that investigates possibilities for implementing consciousness as vital. In an early effort&nbsp;<a title="Igor Aleksander" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Igor_Aleksander">Igor Aleksander</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAleksander1996_126-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAleksander1996-126">[118]</a></sup>&nbsp;argued that the principles for creating a conscious machine already existed but that it would take forty years to train such a machine to understand&nbsp;<a title="Language" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language">language</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template">[<em><a title="Wikipedia:Please clarify" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></em>]</sup></p>
<h2><span id="Research_challenges" class="mw-headline">Research challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Research challenges" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also:&nbsp;<a title="History of artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#The_problems">History of artificial intelligence &sect;&nbsp;The problems</a>, and&nbsp;<a title="History of artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#Predictions_(or_%22Where_is_HAL_9000?%22)">History of artificial intelligence &sect;&nbsp;Predictions (or "Where is HAL 9000?")</a></div>
<p>Progress in artificial intelligence has gone through periods of rapid progress separated by periods when progress appeared to stop.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003_79-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003-79">[75]</a></sup>&nbsp;Ending each hiatus fundamental advances in hardware, software or both to create space for further progress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003_79-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClocksin2003-79">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup>&nbsp;For example, the computer hardware available in the twentieth century was not sufficient to implement deep learning, which requires large numbers of GPU-enabled CPUs.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>The field has also oscillated between approaches to the problem. At times, effort has focused on explicit accumulation of facts and logic, as in&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Expert systems" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_systems">expert systems</a>. At other times, systems were expected to build their own g via machine learning, as in artificial neural networks.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-130">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>A further challenge is the lack of clarity in defining what&nbsp;<a title="Intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligence">intelligence</a>&nbsp;entails. Does it require consciousness? Must it display the ability to set goals as well as pursue them? Is it purely a matter of scale such that if model sizes increase sufficiently, intelligence will emerge? Are facilities such as planning, reasoning, and causal understanding required? Does intelligence require explicitly replicating the brain and its specific faculties? Does it require emotions?<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-131">[123]</a></sup>&nbsp;<a title="David Gelernter" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Gelernter">Gelernter</a>&nbsp;writes, "No computer will be creative unless it can simulate all the nuances of human emotion."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGelernter2010_132-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGelernter2010-132">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-133">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-134">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Benefits" class="mw-headline">Benefits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Benefits" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>AGI could have a wide variety of applications. If oriented towards such goal, AGI could help mitigate various problems in the world such as hunger, poverty and health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-135">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>AGI could improve the productivity and efficiency in most jobs. For example, in public health, AGI could accelerate medical research, notably against cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-136">[128]</a></sup>&nbsp;It could take care of the elderly,<sup id="cite_ref-:8_137-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:8-137">[129]</a></sup>&nbsp;and democratize access to rapid, high-quality medical diagnostics. It could offer fun, cheap and personalized education.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_137-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:8-137">[129]</a></sup>&nbsp;For virtually any job that benefits society if done well, it would probably sooner or later be preferable to leave it to an AGI. The need to work to subsist could become obsolete if the wealth produced is properly redistributed.<sup id="cite_ref-:8_137-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:8-137">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:9_138-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:9-138">[130]</a></sup>&nbsp;This also raises the question of the place of humans in a radically automated society.</p>
<p>AGI could also help to make rational decisions, and to anticipate and prevent disasters. It could also help to reap the benefits of potentially catastrophic technologies such as&nbsp;<a title="Nanotechnology" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanotechnology">nanotechnology</a>&nbsp;or&nbsp;<a title="Climate engineering" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Climate_engineering">climate engineering</a>, while avoiding the associated risks.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_139-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:7-139">[131]</a></sup>&nbsp;If an AGI's primary goal is to prevent existential catastrophes such as human extinction (which could be difficult if the Vulnerable World Hypothesis turns out to be true<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup>), it could take measures to drastically reduce the risks<sup id="cite_ref-:7_139-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:7-139">[131]</a></sup>&nbsp;while minimizing the impact of these measures on our quality of life.</p>
<h2><span id="Risks" class="mw-headline">Risks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Risks" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Potential_threat_to_human_existence" class="mw-headline">Potential threat to human existence<span id="Risk_of_human_extinction" class="anchor"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Potential threat to human existence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles:&nbsp;<a title="Existential risk from artificial general intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_from_artificial_general_intelligence">Existential risk from artificial general intelligence</a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a title="AI safety" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_safety">AI safety</a></div>
<p>The thesis that AI poses an existential risk for humans, and that this risk needs much more attention than it currently gets, is controversial but has been endorsed by many public figures including&nbsp;<a title="Elon Musk" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elon_Musk">Elon Musk</a>,&nbsp;<a title="Bill Gates" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bill_Gates">Bill Gates</a>, and&nbsp;<a title="Stephen Hawking" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>. AI researchers like&nbsp;<a title="Stuart J. Russell" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stuart_J._Russell">Stuart J. Russell</a>,&nbsp;<a title="Roman Yampolskiy" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_Yampolskiy">Roman Yampolskiy</a>, and Alexey Turchin, also support the basic thesis of a potential threat to humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-physica_scripta_124-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-physica_scripta-124">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup>&nbsp;Gates states he does not "understand why some people are not concerned",<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup>&nbsp;and Hawking criticized widespread indifference in his 2014 editorial:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>So, facing possible futures of incalculable benefits and risks, the experts are surely doing everything possible to ensure the best outcome, right? Wrong. If a superior alien civilisation sent us a message saying, 'We'll arrive in a few decades,' would we just reply, 'OK, call us when you get here&ndash;we'll leave the lights on?' Probably not&ndash;but this is more or less what is happening with AI.<sup id="cite_ref-hawking_editorial_144-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-hawking_editorial-144">[136]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The fate of humanity has sometimes been compared to the fate of gorillas threatened by human activities. Additional intelligence caused humanity to dominate gorillas, which are now vulnerable in ways that they could not have anticipated. The gorilla has become an endangered species, not out of malice, but simply as a collateral damage from human activities.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-145">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skeptic&nbsp;<a title="Yann LeCun" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yann_LeCun">Yann LeCun</a>&nbsp;considers that AGIs will have no desire to dominate humanity and that we should be careful not to anthropomorphize them and interpret their intents as we would for humans. He said that people won't be "smart enough to design super-intelligent machines, yet ridiculously stupid to the point of giving it moronic objectives with no safeguards".<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-146">[138]</a></sup>&nbsp;On the other side, the concept of&nbsp;<a title="Instrumental convergence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instrumental_convergence">instrumental convergence</a>&nbsp;suggests that almost whatever their goals,&nbsp;<a title="Intelligent agent" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelligent_agent">intelligent agents</a>&nbsp;will have reasons to try to survive and acquire more power as intermediary steps to achieving these goals. And that this does not require having emotions.&nbsp;<a title="Nick Bostrom" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nick_Bostrom">Nick Bostrom</a>&nbsp;gives the thought experiment of the paper clips optimizer:<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-147">[139]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Suppose we have an AI whose only goal is to make as many paper clips as possible. The AI will realize quickly that it would be much better if there were no humans because humans might decide to switch it off. Because if humans do so, there would be fewer paper clips. Also, human bodies contain a lot of atoms that could be made into paper clips. The future that the AI would be trying to gear towards would be one in which there were a lot of paper clips but no humans.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>A 2021 systematic review of the risks associated with AGI, while noting the paucity of data, found the following potential threats: "AGI removing itself from the control of human owners/managers, being given or developing unsafe goals, development of unsafe AGI, AGIs with poor ethics, morals and values; inadequate management of AGI, and existential risks".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-148">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many scholars who are concerned about existential risk advocate (possibly massive) research into solving the difficult "<a class="mw-redirect" title="AI control problem" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_control_problem">control problem</a>" to answer the question: what types of safeguards, algorithms, or architectures can programmers implement to maximise the probability that their recursively-improving AI would continue to behave in a&nbsp;<a title="Friendly artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friendly_artificial_intelligence">friendly</a>, rather than destructive, manner after it reaches superintelligence?<sup id="cite_ref-physica_scripta_124-2" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-physica_scripta-124">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup>&nbsp;Solving the control problem is complicated by the AI arms race,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup>&nbsp;which will almost certainly see the militarization and weaponization of AGI by more than one nation-state, resulting in AGI-enabled warfare, and in the case of AI misalignment, AGI-directed warfare, potentially against all humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>The thesis that AI can pose existential risk also has detractors. Skeptics sometimes charge that the thesis is crypto-religious, with an irrational belief in the possibility of superintelligence replacing an irrational belief in an omnipotent God.&nbsp;<a title="Jaron Lanier" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jaron_Lanier">Jaron Lanier</a>&nbsp;argued in 2014 that the idea that then-current machines were in any way intelligent is "an illusion" and a "stupendous con" by the wealthy.<sup id="cite_ref-atlantic-but-what_154-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-atlantic-but-what-154">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Much criticism argues that AGI is unlikely in the short term. Computer scientist&nbsp;<a title="Gordon Bell" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gordon_Bell">Gordon Bell</a>&nbsp;argues that the human race will destroy itself before it reaches the&nbsp;<a title="Technological singularity" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Technological_singularity">technological singularity</a>.&nbsp;<a title="Gordon Moore" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gordon_Moore">Gordon Moore</a>, the original proponent of&nbsp;<a class="mw-redirect" title="Moore's Law" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_Law">Moore's Law</a>, declares: "I am a skeptic. I don't believe [a technological singularity] is likely to happen, at least for a long time. And I don't know why I feel that way."<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup>&nbsp;Former&nbsp;<a title="Baidu" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baidu">Baidu</a>&nbsp;Vice President and Chief Scientist&nbsp;<a title="Andrew Ng" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Ng">Andrew Ng</a>&nbsp;said in 2015 that worrying about AI existential risk is "like worrying about overpopulation on Mars when we have not even set foot on the planet yet."<sup id="cite_ref-shermer_156-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-shermer-156">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2023, the CEOs of Google DeepMind, OpenAI and Anthropic, along with other industry leaders and researchers, issued a joint statement asserting that "Mitigating the risk of extinction from AI should be a global priority alongside other societal-scale risks such as pandemics and nuclear war."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-158">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Mass_unemployment" class="mw-headline">Mass unemployment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Mass unemployment" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The job market is already impacted by current large language models. Researchers from OpenAI estimated that "80% of the U.S. workforce could have at least 10% of their work tasks affected [...], while around 19% of workers may see at least 50% of their tasks impacted" by the introduction of large language models like GPT-4.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:6_160-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:6-160">[152]</a></sup>&nbsp;Office workers seem to be the most impacted, notably mathematicians, accountants or web designers.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_160-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:6-160">[152]</a></sup>&nbsp;AGI could increase the autonomy of these systems, their ability to make decisions, to interface with other computer tools, but also to control robotized bodies.</p>
<p>According to Stephen Hawking, this can be beneficial or harmful depending on how the wealth will be redistributed:<sup id="cite_ref-:9_138-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-:9-138">[130]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Everyone can enjoy a life of luxurious leisure if the machine-produced wealth is shared, or most people can end up miserably poor if the machine-owners successfully lobby against wealth redistribution. So far, the trend seems to be toward the second option, with technology driving ever-increasing inequality</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Elon Musk considers that the automation of society will require governments to adopt a&nbsp;<a title="Universal basic income" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_basic_income">universal basic income</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="See_also" class="mw-headline">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: See also" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</ul>
</div>
<h2><span id="Notes" class="mw-headline">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a title="Edit section: Notes" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artificial_general_intelligence&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Searle's_Strong_AI-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-Searle's_Strong_AI_15-0"><span class="cite-accessibility-label">Jump up to:</span><sup><em><strong>a</strong></em></sup></a>&nbsp;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-Searle's_Strong_AI_15-1"><sup><em><strong>b</strong></em></sup></a></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">See below for the origin of the term "strong AI", and see the academic definition of "<a title="Chinese room" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room#Strong_AI">strong AI</a>" and weak AI in the article&nbsp;<a title="Chinese room" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_room">Chinese room</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-18" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">AI founder&nbsp;<a title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)">John McCarthy</a>&nbsp;writes: "we cannot yet characterize in general what kinds of computational procedures we want to call intelligent."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>&nbsp;(For a discussion of some definitions of intelligence used by&nbsp;<a title="Artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>&nbsp;researchers, see&nbsp;<a title="Philosophy of artificial intelligence" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence">philosophy of artificial intelligence</a>.)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-53" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">The&nbsp;<a title="Lighthill report" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lighthill_report">Lighthill report</a>&nbsp;specifically criticized AI's "grandiose objectives" and led the dismantling of AI research in England.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>&nbsp;In the U.S.,&nbsp;<a title="DARPA" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA">DARPA</a>&nbsp;became determined to fund only "mission-oriented direct research, rather than basic undirected research".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENRC1999&quot;Shift_to_Applied_Research_Increases_Investment&quot;_51-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENRC1999%22Shift_to_Applied_Research_Increases_Investment%22-51">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-58" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">As AI founder&nbsp;<a title="John McCarthy (computer scientist)" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist)">John McCarthy</a>&nbsp;writes "it would be a great relief to the rest of the workers in AI if the inventors of new general formalisms would express their hopes in a more guarded form than has sometimes been the case."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-103" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">In "Mind Children"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoravec198861_101-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec198861-101">[97]</a></sup>&nbsp;10<sup>15</sup>&nbsp;cps is used. More recently, in 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoravec1998_102-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec1998-102">[98]</a></sup>&nbsp;Moravec argued for 10<sup>8</sup>&nbsp;MIPS which would roughly correspond to 10<sup>14</sup>&nbsp;cps. Moravec talks in terms of MIPS, not "cps", which is a non-standard term Kurzweil introduced.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-112"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-112" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">In Goertzels' AGI book, Yudkowsky proposes 5 levels of organisation that must be understood &ndash; code/data, sensory modality, concept &amp; category, thought, and deliberation (consciousness) &ndash; in order to use the available hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYudkowsky2006_111-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYudkowsky2006-111">[106]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-118" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">As defined in a standard AI textbook: "The assertion that machines could possibly act intelligently (or, perhaps better, act as if they were intelligent) is called the 'weak AI' hypothesis by philosophers, and the assertion that machines that do so are actually thinking (as opposed to simulating thinking) is called the 'strong AI' hypothesis."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003_100-1" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2003-100">[96]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><strong><a title="Jump up" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_ref-123" aria-label="Jump up">^</a></strong></span>&nbsp;<span class="reference-text">Note that&nbsp;<a title="Consciousness" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Consciousness">consciousness</a>&nbsp;is difficult to define. A popular definition, due to&nbsp;<a title="Thomas Nagel" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Nagel">Thomas Nagel</a>, is that it "feels like" something to be conscious. If we are not conscious, then it doesn't feel like anything. Nagel uses the example of a bat: we can sensibly ask "what does it feel like to be a bat?" However, we are unlikely to ask "what does it feel like to be a toaster?" Nagel concludes that a bat appears to be conscious (i.e. has consciousness) but a toaster does not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENagel1974_122-0" class="reference"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_general_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENagel1974-122">[115]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
